Title: Abiogenesis

URL Source: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis

Published Time: 2001-11-27T02:07:12Z

Markdown Content:
"Origin of life" redirects here. For non-scientific views on the origins of life, see [Creation myth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creation_myth "Creation myth"). For the pre-scientific conception of macro-scale organisms randomly arising from non-living materials, see [Spontaneous generation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spontaneous_generation "Spontaneous generation").

[![Image 1](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Origin_of_life_stages.svg/500px-Origin_of_life_stages.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Origin_of_life_stages.svg)

Stages in the origin of life range from the well-understood, such as the [habitable Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability "Planetary habitability") and the abiotic synthesis of simple molecules, to the largely unknown, like the derivation of the [last universal common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") (LUCA) with its complex molecular functionalities.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Walker_Packard_Cody_2017-1)

**Abiogenesis** is the natural process by which [life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life "Life") arises from [non-living matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiotic_component "Abiotic component"), such as simple [organic compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organic_compound "Organic compound"). The prevailing scientific [hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypothesis "Hypothesis") is that the transition from non-living to [living entities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organism "Organism") on Earth was not a single event, but a process of increasing complexity involving the formation of a [habitable planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability "Planetary habitability"), the prebiotic synthesis of organic molecules, molecular [self-replication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-replication "Self-replication"), [self-assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-assembly "Self-assembly"), [autocatalysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocatalysis "Autocatalysis"), and the emergence of [cell membranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_membrane "Cell membrane"). The transition from non-life to life has not been observed experimentally, but many proposals have been made for different stages of the process.

The study of abiogenesis aims to determine how pre-life [chemical reactions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_reaction "Chemical reaction") gave rise to life under conditions strikingly different from those on Earth today. It primarily uses tools from [biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology "Biology") and [chemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemistry "Chemistry"), with more recent approaches attempting a synthesis of many sciences. Life functions through the specialized chemistry of [carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon") and water, and builds largely upon four key families of chemicals: [lipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipid "Lipid") for cell membranes, [carbohydrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbohydrate "Carbohydrate") such as sugars, [amino acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amino_acid "Amino acid") for protein metabolism, and the [nucleic acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleic_acid "Nucleic acid")[DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA") and [RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA "RNA") for the mechanisms of heredity ([genetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetics "Genetics")). Any successful theory of abiogenesis must explain the origins and interactions of these classes of molecules.

Many approaches to abiogenesis investigate how self-replicating molecules, or their components, came into existence. Researchers generally think that current life descends from an [RNA world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_world "RNA world"), although other self-replicating and self-catalyzing molecules may have preceded RNA. Other approaches (["metabolism-first" hypotheses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-metabolism "Proto-metabolism")) focus on understanding how [catalysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalysis "Catalysis") in chemical systems on the early Earth might have provided the [precursor molecules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precursor_(chemistry) "Precursor (chemistry)") necessary for self-replication. The classic 1952 [Miller–Urey experiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment "Miller–Urey experiment") demonstrated that most amino acids, the chemical constituents of [proteins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein "Protein"), can be synthesized from [inorganic compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inorganic_compound "Inorganic compound") under conditions intended to replicate those of the [early Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prebiotic_atmosphere "Prebiotic atmosphere"). External sources of energy may have triggered these reactions, including [lightning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lightning "Lightning"), [radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiation "Radiation"), atmospheric entries of micro-meteorites, and implosion of bubbles in sea and ocean waves. More recent research has found amino acids in meteorites, comets, asteroids, and star-forming regions of space.

While the [last universal common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") of all modern organisms (LUCA) is thought to have existed long after the origin of life, investigations into LUCA can guide research into early universal characteristics. A [genomics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genomics "Genomics") approach has sought to characterize LUCA by identifying the genes shared by [Archaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea "Archaea") and [Bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria"), members of the two major branches of life (with [Eukaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryote "Eukaryote") included in the archaean branch in the [two-domain system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Two-domain_system "Two-domain system")). It appears there are 60 proteins common to all life and [355 prokaryotic genes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#Phylogeny_and_LUCA) that trace to LUCA; their functions imply that the LUCA was [anaerobic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration "Anaerobic respiration") with the [Wood–Ljungdahl pathway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway "Wood–Ljungdahl pathway"), deriving energy by [chemiosmosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemiosmosis "Chemiosmosis"), and maintaining its hereditary material with DNA, the [genetic code](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_code "Genetic code"), and [ribosomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribosome "Ribosome"). Although the LUCA lived over 4 billion years ago (4[Gya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Billion_years "Billion years")), researchers believe it was far from the first form of life. Most evidence suggests that earlier cells might have had a leaky membrane and been powered by a naturally occurring [proton gradient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton_gradient "Proton gradient") near a deep-sea white smoker [hydrothermal vent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent "Hydrothermal vent"); however, other evidence suggests instead that life may have originated inside the continental crust or in water at Earth's surface.

Earth remains the only place in the [universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe") known to harbor life. [Geochemical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms "Earliest known life forms") and [fossil evidence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stromatolite "Stromatolite") from the Earth informs most studies of abiogenesis. The [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Earth "Age of Earth") was formed at 4.54 Gya, and the earliest evidence of life on Earth dates from at least 3.8 Gya from [Western Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dresser_Formation "Dresser Formation"). Some studies have suggested that [fossil micro-organisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micropaleontology#Microfossils "Micropaleontology") may have lived within hydrothermal vent precipitates dated 3.77 to 4.28 Gya [from Quebec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuvvuagittuq_Greenstone_Belt "Nuvvuagittuq Greenstone Belt"), soon after [ocean formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#History_of_water_on_Earth "Origin of water on Earth") 4.4 Gya during the [Hadean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadean "Hadean").

[![Image 2](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NASA_on_astrobiology.svg/500px-NASA_on_astrobiology.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA_on_astrobiology.svg)

NASA's 2015 strategy for [astrobiology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrobiology "Astrobiology") aimed to solve the puzzle of the origin of life – how a fully functioning living system could emerge from non-living components – through research on the prebiotic origin of [life's chemicals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biochemistry "Biochemistry"), both in [space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_space "Outer space") and on [planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet "Planet"), as well as the functioning of early biomolecules to [catalyse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalysis "Catalysis") reactions and support [inheritance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_code "Genetic code").[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-2)

[Life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life#Definitions "Life") consists of reproduction with (heritable) variations.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-3)[NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") defines life as "a self-sustaining chemical system capable of [Darwinian evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution "Evolution")."[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-4) Such a system is complex; the [last universal common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") (LUCA), presumably a single-celled organism which lived some 4 billion years ago, already had hundreds of [genes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene "Gene") encoded in the [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA")[genetic code](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_code "Genetic code") that is universal today. That in turn implies a suite of cellular machinery including [messenger RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Messenger_RNA "Messenger RNA"), [transfer RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transfer_RNA "Transfer RNA"), and [ribosomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribosome "Ribosome") to translate the code into [proteins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein "Protein"). Those proteins included [enzymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enzyme "Enzyme") to operate its [anaerobic respiration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration "Anaerobic respiration") via the [Wood–Ljungdahl metabolic pathway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway "Wood–Ljungdahl pathway"), and a [DNA polymerase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_polymerase "DNA polymerase") to replicate its genetic material.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Witzany_2016-5)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AB-20141208-6)

The challenge for abiogenesis (origin of life)[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-7)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Pereto-8)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-9) researchers is to explain how such a complex and tightly interlinked system could develop by evolutionary steps, as at first sight [all its parts are necessary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irreducible_complexity "Irreducible complexity") to enable it to function. For example, a cell, whether the LUCA or in a modern organism, copies its DNA with the DNA polymerase enzyme, which is itself produced by translating the DNA polymerase gene in the DNA. Neither the enzyme nor the DNA can be produced without the other.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Weiss_Sousa_Mrnjavac_2016-10) The likely answer to this challenge is that the evolutionary process could have started with molecular [self-replication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-replication "Self-replication"), [self-assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-assembly "Self-assembly") such as of [cell membranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_membrane "Cell membrane"), and [autocatalysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocatalysis "Autocatalysis") via RNA [ribozymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribozyme "Ribozyme") in an [RNA world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_world "RNA world") environment.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Witzany_2016-5)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AB-20141208-6)[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-11) Nonetheless, the transition of non-life to life has never been observed experimentally, nor has there been a satisfactory chemical explanation.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-12)

The preconditions to the development of a living cell like the LUCA are known, though disputed in detail: a habitable world is formed with a supply of minerals and liquid water. Prebiotic synthesis creates a range of simple organic compounds, which are assembled into polymers such as proteins and RNA. On the other side, the process after the LUCA is readily understood: biological evolution caused the development of a wide range of species with varied forms and biochemical capabilities. However, the derivation of the LUCA from simple components is far from understood.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Walker_Packard_Cody_2017-1)

Although Earth remains the only place where life is known,[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-13)[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-14) the science of [astrobiology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrobiology "Astrobiology") seeks evidence of life on other planets. The 2015 NASA strategy on the origin of life aimed to solve the puzzle by identifying interactions, intermediary structures and functions, energy sources, and environmental factors that contributed to evolvable macromolecular systems,[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-2) and mapping the chemical landscape of potential primordial informational [polymers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymer "Polymer"). The advent of such polymers was most likely a critical step in prebiotic chemical evolution.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-2) Those polymers derived, in turn, from simple [organic compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organic_compound "Organic compound") such as [nucleobases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleobase "Nucleobase"), [amino acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amino_acid "Amino acid"), and [sugars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sugar "Sugar"), likely formed by reactions in the environment.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-15)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Pereto-8)[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-16)[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-17) A successful theory of the origin of life must explain how all these chemicals came into being.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-18)

Pre-1960s conceptual history
----------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Pre-1960s conceptual history")]

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg/500px-Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Miller-Urey_experiment-en.svg)

The [Miller–Urey experiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment "Miller–Urey experiment") was a synthesis of small organic molecules in a mixture of simple gases in a thermal gradient created by heating (right) and cooling (left) the mixture at the same time, with electrical discharges.

### Spontaneous generation

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Spontaneous generation")]

One ancient view of the origin of life, from [Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology "Aristotle's biology") until the 19th century, was of [spontaneous generation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spontaneous_generation "Spontaneous generation").[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-19) This held that "lower" animals such as insects were generated by decaying organic substances, and that life arose by chance.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-20)[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Bernal_1967-21) This was questioned from the 17th century, in works like [Thomas Browne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Browne "Sir Thomas Browne")'s _[Pseudodoxia Epidemica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudodoxia\_Epidemica "Pseudodoxia Epidemica")_.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-22)[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-23) In 1665, [Robert Hooke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Hooke "Robert Hooke") published the first drawings of a [microorganism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microorganism "Microorganism"). In 1676, [Antonie van Leeuwenhoek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek "Antonie van Leeuwenhoek") drew and described microorganisms, probably [protozoa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protozoa "Protozoa") and [bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-24) Van Leeuwenhoek disagreed with spontaneous generation, and by the 1680s convinced himself, using experiments ranging from sealed and open meat incubation and the close study of insect reproduction, that the theory was incorrect.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-25) In 1668 [Francesco Redi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francesco_Redi "Francesco Redi") showed that no [maggots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maggot "Maggot") appeared in meat when flies were prevented from laying eggs.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-26) By the middle of the 19th century, spontaneous generation was considered disproven.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-27)[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-28)

Dating back to [Anaxagoras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaxagoras "Anaxagoras") in the 5th century BC, [panspermia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panspermia "Panspermia")[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-29) is the idea that [life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life "Life") originated elsewhere in the [universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe") and came to Earth. The modern version of panspermia holds that life may have been distributed to Earth by [meteoroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteoroids "Meteoroids"), [asteroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroids "Asteroids"), [comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comets "Comets")[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-30) or [planetoids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body "Small Solar System body").[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-31) This shifts the origin of life to another heavenly body. The advantage is that life is not required to have formed on each planet it occurs on, but in a more limited set of locations, and then spread about the [galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy "Galaxy") to other star systems.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-32) There is some interest in the possibility that [life originated on Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life_on_Mars "Life on Mars") and later transferred to Earth.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-33)

### "A warm little pond": primordial soup

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: \"A warm little pond\": primordial soup")]

The idea that life originated from non-living matter in slow stages appeared in [Herbert Spencer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_Spencer "Herbert Spencer")'s 1864–1867 book _Principles of Biology_, and in [William Turner Thiselton-Dyer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Turner_Thiselton-Dyer "William Turner Thiselton-Dyer")'s 1879 paper "On spontaneous generation and evolution". On 1 February 1871 [Charles Darwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Darwin "Charles Darwin") wrote about these publications to [Joseph Hooker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Dalton_Hooker "Joseph Dalton Hooker"), and set out his own speculation that the original spark of life may have been in a "warm little pond, with all sorts of [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia") and phosphoric [salts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt_(chemistry) "Salt (chemistry)"),—light, heat, electricity &c present, that a protein compound was chemically formed". Darwin explained that "at the present day such matter would be instantly devoured or absorbed, which would not have been the case before living creatures were formed."[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-34)[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-35)[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-36)

[Alexander Oparin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Oparin "Alexander Oparin") in 1924 and [J. B. S. Haldane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._B._S._Haldane "J. B. S. Haldane") in 1929 proposed that the earliest cells slowly self-organized from a [primordial soup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primordial_soup "Primordial soup"), the [Oparin–Haldane hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primordial_soup "Primordial soup").[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-37)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-38) Haldane suggested that the Earth's prebiotic oceans consisted of a "hot dilute soup" in which organic compounds could have formed.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Bernal_1967-21)[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-39)[J. D. Bernal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._D._Bernal "J. D. Bernal") showed that such mechanisms could form most of the necessary molecules for life from inorganic precursors.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-40) In 1967, he suggested three "stages": the origin of biological [monomers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monomer "Monomer"); the origin of biological [polymers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymer "Polymer"); and the evolution from molecules to cells.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Martin2003-41)[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-42)

### Miller–Urey experiment

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: Miller–Urey experiment")]

In 1952, [Stanley Miller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanley_L._Miller "Stanley L. Miller") and [Harold Urey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harold_C._Urey "Harold C. Urey") carried out a chemical experiment to demonstrate how organic molecules could have formed spontaneously from inorganic precursors under [prebiotic conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prebiotic_atmosphere "Prebiotic atmosphere") like those posited by the Oparin–Haldane hypothesis. It used a highly [reducing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Redox "Redox") (lacking oxygen) mixture of gases—[methane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methane "Methane"), [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia"), and [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_gas "Hydrogen gas"), with [water vapor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapor "Water vapor")—to form organic monomers such as [amino acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amino_acid "Amino acid").[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-43)[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-44) Bernal said of the Miller–Urey experiment that "it is not enough to explain the formation of such molecules, what is necessary, is a physical-chemical explanation of the origins of these molecules that suggests the presence of suitable sources and sinks for free energy."[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-45) However, current scientific consensus describes the primitive atmosphere as weakly reducing or neutral,[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Cleaves_2008-46)[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-47) diminishing the amount and variety of amino acids that could be produced. The addition of [iron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron "Iron") and [carbonate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbonate "Carbonate") minerals, present in early oceans, produces a diverse array of amino acids.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Cleaves_2008-46) Later work has focused on two other potential reducing environments: [outer space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_space "Outer space") and deep-sea hydrothermal vents.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-48)[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-49)[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-50)

Producing a habitable Earth
---------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: Producing a habitable Earth")]

### Evolutionary history

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: Evolutionary history")]

#### Early universe with first stars

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Early universe with first stars")]

Soon after the [Big Bang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang "Big Bang"), roughly 14 Gya, the only chemical elements present in the universe were [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen"), [helium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium "Helium"), and [lithium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithium "Lithium"), the three lightest atoms in the periodic table. These elements gradually accreted and began orbiting in disks of gas and dust. Gravitational accretion of material at the hot and dense centers of these [protoplanetary disks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk "Protoplanetary disk") formed stars by the fusion of hydrogen.[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-51) Early stars were massive and short-lived, producing all the heavier elements by [stellar nucleosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis "Stellar nucleosynthesis"). Such element formation proceeds to its most stable element Iron-[56](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotopes_of_iron "Isotopes of iron"). Heavier elements were formed during supernovae at the end of a star's lifecycle. [Carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon"), currently the [fourth most abundant element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements "Abundance of the chemical elements") in the universe, was formed mainly in [white dwarf stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_dwarf_stars "White dwarf stars").[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-52) As these stars reached the end of their [lifecycles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellar_life_cycle "Stellar life cycle"), they ejected heavier elements, including carbon and oxygen, throughout the universe. These allowed for the formation of rocky planets.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-53) According to the [nebular hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis "Nebular hypothesis"), the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System") began to form 4.6 Gya with the [gravitational collapse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_collapse "Gravitational collapse") of part of a giant [molecular cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_cloud "Molecular cloud"). Most of the collapsing mass collected in the center, forming the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun"), while the rest flattened into a [protoplanetary disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk "Protoplanetary disk") out of which the [planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet "Planet") formed.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-54)

The [age of the Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth "Age of the Earth") is 4.54 Gya as found by radiometric dating of [calcium-aluminium-rich inclusions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calcium%E2%80%93aluminium-rich_inclusion "Calcium–aluminium-rich inclusion") in [carbonaceous chrondrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbonaceous_chrondrite&action=edit&redlink=1 "Carbonaceous chrondrite (page does not exist)") meteorites, the oldest material in the Solar System.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-55)[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-56) Earth, during the [Hadean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadean "Hadean") eon (from its formation until 4.031 Gya,) was at first inhospitable to life. During its formation, the Earth lost much of its initial mass, and so lacked the [gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity "Gravity") to hold molecular hydrogen and the bulk of the original inert gases.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-57) Soon after initial accretion of Earth at 4.48 Gya, its collision with [Theia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theia_(planet) "Theia (planet)"), a hypothesised impactor, is thought to have created the ejected debris that eventually formed the Moon.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-58) This impact removed the Earth's primary atmosphere, leaving behind clouds of viscous silicates and carbon dioxide. This unstable atmosphere was short-lived, soon condensing to form the bulk silicate Earth, leaving behind an atmosphere largely consisting of water vapor, [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen"), and [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide"), with smaller amounts of [carbon monoxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_monoxide "Carbon monoxide"), hydrogen, and [sulfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur") compounds.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-59)[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Follmann2009-60) The solution of carbon dioxide in water is thought to have made the seas slightly [acidic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid "Acid"), with a [pH](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PH "PH") of about 5.5.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-61)

Condensation to form liquid [oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth "Origin of water on Earth") is theorised to have occurred as early as the Moon-forming impact.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-62)[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-63) This scenario is supported by the dating of 4.404 Gya [zircon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zircon "Zircon") crystals with high [_δ_ 18 O](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%9418O "Δ18O") values from metamorphosed [quartzite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quartzite "Quartzite") of [Mount Narryer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Narryer_Gneiss_terrane "Narryer Gneiss terrane") in Western Australia.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-64)[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-65) The Hadean atmosphere has been characterized as a "gigantic, productive outdoor chemical laboratory," similar to volcanic gases today which still support some abiotic chemistry. Despite the likely increased volcanism from early plate tectonics, the Earth may have been a predominantly water world between 4.4 and 4.3 Gya. It is debated whether crust was exposed above this ocean.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-66) Immediately after the Moon-forming impact, Earth likely had little if any continental crust, a turbulent atmosphere, and a [hydrosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrosphere "Hydrosphere") subject to intense [ultraviolet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet "Ultraviolet") light from a [T Tauri stage Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T_Tauri_star "T Tauri star"). It was also affected by [cosmic radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_ray "Cosmic ray"), and continued asteroid and [comet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet "Comet") impacts.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-67)

The [Late Heavy Bombardment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment "Late Heavy Bombardment") hypothesis posits that a period of intense impact occurred at 4.1 to 3.8 Gya during the Hadean and early [Archean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archean "Archean") eons.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-68)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-69) Originally it was thought that the Late Heavy Bombardment was a single cataclysmic impact event occurring at 3.9 Gya; this would have had the potential to sterilise Earth by volatilising liquid oceans and blocking sunlight needed for photosynthesis, delaying the earliest possible emergence of life.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-70) More recent research questioned the intensity of the Late Heavy Bombardment and its potential for sterilisation. If it was not one giant impact but a period of raised impact rate, it would have had much less destructive power.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-71)[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-72) The 3.9 Gya date arose from dating of [Apollo mission sample returns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_program "Apollo program") collected mostly near the [Imbrium Basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Imbrium "Mare Imbrium"), biasing the age of recorded impacts.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-73) Impact modelling of the lunar surface reveals that rather than a cataclysmic event at 3.9 Gya, multiple small-scale, short-lived periods of bombardment likely occurred.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-74) Terrestrial data backs this idea by showing multiple periods of ejecta in the rock record both before and after the 3.9 Gya marker, suggesting that the early Earth was subject to continuous impacts with less impact on extinction.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-75)

If life evolved in the ocean at depths of more than ten meters, it would have been shielded both from late impacts and the then high levels of ultraviolet radiation from the sun. Geothermically heated oceanic crust could have yielded far more organic compounds through deep [hydrothermal vents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent "Hydrothermal vent") than the [Miller–Urey experiments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment "Miller–Urey experiment") indicated.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-76) The available energy is maximized at 100–150°C, the temperatures at which [hyperthermophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperthermophile "Hyperthermophile") bacteria and [thermoacidophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoacidophile "Thermoacidophile")[archaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea "Archaea") live.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-77)

#### Earliest evidence of life

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Earliest evidence of life")]

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Stromatolithe_Pal%C3%A9oarch%C3%A9en_-_MNHT.PAL.2009.10.1.jpg/500px-Stromatolithe_Pal%C3%A9oarch%C3%A9en_-_MNHT.PAL.2009.10.1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Stromatolithe_Pal%C3%A9oarch%C3%A9en_-_MNHT.PAL.2009.10.1.jpg)

If [banded iron formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banded_iron_formation "Banded iron formation") rocks of Archaean age (like these from Australia) are fossilized [stromatolites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stromatolite "Stromatolite"), they would be among the earliest life-forms.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NAT-20170301-78)[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Garc%C3%ADa-Ruiz_2017-79)

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg/500px-Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Stromatolites_in_Sharkbay.jpg)

Modern stromatolites in [Shark Bay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shark_Bay "Shark Bay"), created by photosynthetic [cyanobacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanobacteria "Cyanobacteria")

Life most likely emerged on Earth between 3.48 and 4.32 Gya. Minimum age estimates are based on evidence from the [geologic record](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geologic_record "Geologic record"). In 2017, the earliest physical evidence of life was reported to consist of [microbialites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microbialite "Microbialite") in the [Nuvvuagittuq Greenstone Belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuvvuagittuq_Greenstone_Belt "Nuvvuagittuq Greenstone Belt") of Northern Quebec, in [banded iron formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banded_iron_formation "Banded iron formation") rocks at least 3.77 and possibly as old as 4.32 Gya. The micro-organisms could have lived within hydrothermal vent precipitates, soon after the 4.4 Gya [formation of oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#History_of_water_on_Earth "Origin of water on Earth") during the Hadean. The microbes resemble modern hydrothermal vent bacteria, supporting the view that abiogenesis began in such an environment.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NAT-20170301-78) However, later research disputed this interpretation of the data, stating that the observations may be better explained by abiotic processes in silica-rich waters,[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Garc%C3%ADa-Ruiz_2017-79) "chemical gardens,"[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-80) circulating hydrothermal fluids,[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-81) or volcanic ejecta.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-82)

Biogenic [graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite") has been found in 3.7 Gya metasedimentary rocks from southwestern [Greenland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenland "Greenland")[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-83) and in [microbial mat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microbial_mat "Microbial mat") fossils from 3.49 Gya [cherts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chert "Chert") in the [Pilbara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pilbara_craton "Pilbara craton") region of [Western Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Australia "Western Australia").[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AST-20131108-84) Evidence of early life in rocks from [Akilia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akilia "Akilia") Island, near the [Isua supracrustal belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isua_Greenstone_Belt "Isua Greenstone Belt") in southwestern Greenland, dating to 3.7 Gya, have shown biogenic [carbon isotopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_isotope "Carbon isotope").[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-85) In other parts of the Isua supracrustal belt, graphite inclusions trapped within [garnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Garnet "Garnet") crystals are connected to the other elements of life: oxygen, nitrogen, and possibly phosphorus in the form of [phosphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phosphate "Phosphate"), providing further evidence for life 3.7 Gya.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-86) In the [Pilbara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pilbara "Pilbara") region of Western Australia, compelling evidence of early life was found in [pyrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrite "Pyrite")-bearing sandstone in a fossilized beach, with rounded tubular cells that oxidized sulfur by photosynthesis in the absence of oxygen.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-87)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-88) Carbon isotope ratios on graphite inclusions from the Jack Hills zircons suggest that life could have existed on Earth from 4.1 Gya.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-89)

The Pilbara region of Western Australia contains the [Dresser Formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dresser_Formation "Dresser Formation") with rocks 3.48 Gya, including layered structures called [stromatolites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stromatolite "Stromatolite"). Their modern counterparts are created by photosynthetic micro-organisms including [cyanobacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanobacteria "Cyanobacteria").[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-90) These lie within undeformed hydrothermal-sedimentary strata; their texture indicates a biogenic origin. Parts of the Dresser formation preserve [hot springs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hot_spring "Hot spring") on land, but other regions seem to have been shallow seas.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Djokic_Van_Kranendonk_2017-91) A molecular clock analysis suggests the LUCA emerged prior to 3.9 Gya.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-92)

Producing molecules: prebiotic synthesis
----------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Producing molecules: prebiotic synthesis")]

All [chemical elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_element "Chemical element") derive from stellar nucleosynthesis except for hydrogen and some helium and lithium. Basic chemical ingredients of life – the [carbon-hydrogen molecule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon-hydrogen_bond "Carbon-hydrogen bond") (CH), the carbon-hydrogen positive ion (CH+) and the carbon ion (C+) – can be produced by [ultraviolet light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet_light "Ultraviolet light") from stars.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA-20161012-93) Complex molecules, including organic molecules, form naturally both in space and on planets.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Ehrenfreund2010-94) Organic molecules on the early Earth could have had either terrestrial origins, with organic molecule synthesis driven by impact shocks or by other energy sources, such as ultraviolet light, [redox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organic_redox_reaction "Organic redox reaction") coupling, or electrical discharges; or extraterrestrial origins ([pseudo-panspermia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panspermia#Origination_and_distribution_of_organic_molecules:_Pseudo-panspermia "Panspermia")), with organic molecules formed in [interstellar dust clouds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_cloud "Interstellar cloud") raining down on to the planet.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-95)[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-96)

An organic compound is a chemical whose molecules contain carbon. Carbon is abundant in the Sun, stars, comets, and in the [atmospheres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere "Atmosphere") of most planets of the Solar System.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA-20140221-97) Organic compounds are relatively common in space, formed by "factories of complex molecular synthesis" which occur in molecular clouds and [circumstellar envelopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumstellar_envelope "Circumstellar envelope"), and chemically evolve after reactions are initiated mostly by [ionizing radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionizing_radiation "Ionizing radiation").[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Ehrenfreund2010-94)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-98)[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-99)[Purine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Purine "Purine") and [pyrimidine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrimidine "Pyrimidine") nucleobases including [guanine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guanine "Guanine"), [adenine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adenine "Adenine"), [cytosine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytosine "Cytosine"), [uracil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uracil "Uracil"), and [thymine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thymine "Thymine"), as well as [sugars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sugar_phosphates "Sugar phosphates"), have been found in [meteorites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorite "Meteorite"). These could have provided the materials for DNA and [RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA "RNA") to form on the [early Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Earth "Early Earth").[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NC-20220426-100)[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-101) The amino acid [glycine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glycine "Glycine") was found in material ejected from comet [Wild 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/81P/Wild "81P/Wild"); it had earlier been detected in meteorites.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-102) Comets are encrusted with dark material, thought to be a [tar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tar "Tar")-like organic substance formed from simple carbon compounds under ionizing radiation. A rain of material from comets could have brought such complex organic molecules to Earth.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-103)[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-104)[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Follmann2009-60) It is estimated that during the Late Heavy Bombardment, meteorites may have delivered up to five million [tons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ton "Ton") of organic prebiotic elements to Earth per year.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Follmann2009-60) Currently 40,000 tons of cosmic dust falls to Earth each year.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-105)

#### Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons")]

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg/330px-PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PIA22568-CatsPawNebula-Spitzer-20181023.jpg)

The [Cat's Paw Nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cat%27s_Paw_Nebula "Cat's Paw Nebula") is inside the [Milky Way Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way_Galaxy "Milky Way Galaxy"), in the [constellation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constellation "Constellation")[Scorpius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scorpius "Scorpius").

Green areas show regions where radiation from hot stars collided with large molecules and small dust grains called "[polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon "Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon")" (PAHs), causing them to [fluoresce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluoresce "Fluoresce"). [Spitzer Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope "Spitzer Space Telescope"), 2018.

[Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon "Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon") (PAH) are the most common and abundant polyatomic molecules in the [observable universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observable_universe "Observable universe"), and are a major store of carbon.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA-20140221-97)[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-106)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AJ-20051010-107)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-CD-108) They seem to have formed shortly after the Big Bang,[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-SP-20051018-109)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AJ-20051010-107)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-CD-108) and are associated with [new stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_formation "Star formation") and [exoplanets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoplanet "Exoplanet").[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA-20140221-97) They are a likely constituent of Earth's primordial sea.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-SP-20051018-109)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AJ-20051010-107)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-CD-108) PAHs have been detected in [nebulae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nebula "Nebula"),[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AJL-20101120-110) and in the [interstellar medium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interstellar_medium "Interstellar medium"), in comets, and in meteorites.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA-20140221-97)

A star, HH 46-IR, resembling the sun early in its life, is surrounded by a disk of material which contains molecules including cyanide compounds, [hydrocarbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrocarbon "Hydrocarbon"), and carbon monoxide. PAHs in the interstellar medium can be transformed through [hydrogenation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogenation "Hydrogenation"), [oxygenation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygenate "Oxygenate"), and [hydroxylation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydroxylation "Hydroxylation") to more complex organic compounds used in living cells.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AJL-20120901-111)

#### Nucleobases and nucleotides

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Nucleobases and nucleotides")]

Organic compounds introduced on Earth by [interstellar dust particles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_dust "Cosmic dust") can help to form complex molecules, thanks to their peculiar [surface-catalytic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalysis "Catalysis") activities.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lincei-112)[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-113) The RNA component uracil and related molecules, including [xanthine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xanthine "Xanthine"), in the [Murchison meteorite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murchison_meteorite "Murchison meteorite") were likely formed extraterrestrially, as suggested by studies of 12 C/13 C [isotopic ratios](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_abundance "Natural abundance").[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Murch_base-114) NASA studies of meteorites suggest that all four DNA nucleobases (adenine, guanine and related organic molecules) have been formed in outer space.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lincei-112)[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Callahan-115)[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Steigerwald-116) The [cosmic dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_dust "Cosmic dust") permeating the universe contains complex organics ("amorphous organic solids with a mixed [aromatic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aromaticity "Aromaticity")–[aliphatic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aliphatic_compound "Aliphatic compound") structure") that could be created rapidly by stars.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Nature-20111026-117)[Glycolaldehyde](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glycolaldehyde "Glycolaldehyde"), a sugar molecule and RNA precursor, has been detected in regions of space including around [protostars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protostar "Protostar") and on meteorites.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-118)[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-PNAS-20191113-119)

### Laboratory synthesis

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Laboratory synthesis")]

As early as the 1860s, experiments demonstrated that biologically relevant molecules can be produced from interaction of simple carbon sources with abundant inorganic catalysts. The spontaneous formation of complex polymers from abiotically generated monomers under the conditions posited by the "soup" theory is not straightforward. Besides the necessary basic organic monomers, compounds that would have prohibited the formation of polymers were also formed in high concentration during the [Miller–Urey experiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment "Miller–Urey experiment") and [Joan Oró](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joan_Or%C3%B3 "Joan Oró") experiments.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-120) Biology uses essentially 20 amino acids for its coded protein enzymes, representing a very small subset of the structurally possible products. Since life tends to use whatever is available, an explanation is needed for why the set used is so small.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-121) Formamide is attractive as a medium that potentially provided a source of amino acid derivatives from simple aldehyde and nitrile feedstocks.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-122)

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Formose.png/500px-Formose.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Formose.png)

The Breslow catalytic cycle for formaldehyde dimerization and C2-C6 sugar formation

[Alexander Butlerov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Butlerov "Alexander Butlerov") showed in 1861 that the [formose reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formose_reaction "Formose reaction") created sugars including tetroses, pentoses, and hexoses when [formaldehyde](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formaldehyde "Formaldehyde") is heated under basic conditions with divalent metal ions like calcium. R. Breslow proposed that the reaction was autocatalytic in 1959.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-123)

Nucleobases, such as guanine and adenine, can be synthesized from simple carbon and nitrogen sources, such as [hydrogen cyanide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide "Hydrogen cyanide") (HCN) and ammonia.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-124)[Formamide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formamide "Formamide") produces all four ribonucleotides when warmed with terrestrial minerals. It is ubiquitous, produced by the reaction of water and HCN. It can be concentrated by the evaporation of water.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Saladino2012-125)[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Saladino2012b-126) HCN is poisonous only to [aerobic organisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerobic_organism "Aerobic organism"), which did not yet exist. It can contribute to chemical processes such as the synthesis of the amino acid glycine.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Follmann2009-60)

DNA and RNA components including uracil, cytosine and thymine can be synthesized under outer space conditions, using starting chemicals such as pyrimidine found in meteorites. Pyrimidine may have been formed in [red giant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_giant "Red giant") stars or in interstellar dust and gas clouds.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA-20150303-127) All four RNA-bases may be synthesized from formamide in high-energy density events like extraterrestrial impacts.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-128) Several ribonucleotides for RNA formation have been synthesized in a laboratory environment which replicates [prebiotic conditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prebiotic_world "Prebiotic world") via [autocatalytic formose reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocatalysis "Autocatalysis").[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-129)

Other pathways for synthesizing bases from inorganic materials have been reported.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Basile1984-130) Freezing temperatures assist the synthesis of purines, by concentrating key precursors such as HCN.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-131) However, while adenine and guanine require freezing conditions, cytosine and uracil may require boiling temperatures.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-132) Seven amino acids and eleven types of nucleobases formed in ice when ammonia and [cyanide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanide "Cyanide") were left in a freezer for 25 years.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-133)[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-jeffreybada-134) S-[triazines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triazine "Triazine") (alternative nucleobases), pyrimidines including cytosine and uracil, and adenine can be synthesized by subjecting a urea solution to freeze-thaw cycles under a reductive atmosphere with spark discharges.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-135) The unusual speed of these low-temperature reactions is due to [eutectic freezing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eutectic_system "Eutectic system"), which crowds impurities in microscopic pockets of liquid within the ice.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-136)

Prebiotic peptide synthesis could have occurred by several routes. Some center on high temperature/concentration conditions in which condensation becomes energetically favorable, while others use plausible prebiotic condensing agents.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-:0-137)

Experimental evidence for the formation of peptides in uniquely concentrated environments is bolstered by work suggesting that wet-dry cycles and the presence of specific salts can greatly increase spontaneous condensation of glycine into poly-glycine chains.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-138) Other work suggests that while mineral surfaces, such as those of pyrite, calcite, and rutile catalyze peptide condensation, they also catalyze their hydrolysis. The authors suggest that additional chemical activation or coupling would be necessary to produce peptides at sufficient concentrations. Thus, mineral surface catalysis, while important, is not sufficient alone for peptide synthesis.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-139)

Many prebiotically plausible condensing/activating agents have been identified, including the following: cyanamide, dicyanamide, dicyandiamide, diaminomaleonitrile, urea, trimetaphosphate, NaCl, CuCl 2, (Ni,Fe)S, CO, carbonyl sulfide (COS), carbon disulfide (CS 2), SO 2, and diammonium phosphate (DAP).[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-:0-137)

A 2024 experiment used a sapphire substrate with a web of thin cracks under a heat flow, mimicking [deep-ocean vents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent "Hydrothermal vent"), to concentrate prebiotically-relevant building blocks from a dilute mixture by up to three orders of magnitude. This could help to create biopolymers such as peptides.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-140) A similar role has been suggested for clays.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-141)

The prebiotic synthesis of peptides from simpler molecules such as CO, NH 3 and C, skipping the step of amino acid formation, is also very efficient.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-142)[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-143)

Producing protocells
--------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Producing protocells")]

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg/250px-Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Phospholipids_aqueous_solution_structures.svg)

The three main structures composed of [phospholipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phospholipid "Phospholipid") form spontaneously by [self-assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-assembly "Self-assembly") in solution: the [liposome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liposome "Liposome") (a closed bilayer), the [micelle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micelle "Micelle") and the bilayer.

The largest unanswered question in evolution is how simple protocells first arose and differed in reproductive contribution to the following generation, thus initiating evolution. The [lipid world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gard_model "Gard model") theory postulates that the first self-replicating object was [lipid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipid "Lipid")-like.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-144)[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-145) Phospholipids form [lipid bilayers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipid_bilayer "Lipid bilayer") (as in cell membranes) in water while under agitation. These molecules were not present on early Earth, but other membrane-forming [amphiphilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amphiphile "Amphiphile") long-chain molecules were. These bodies may expand by insertion of additional lipids, and may spontaneously split into two [offspring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Offspring "Offspring") of similar size and composition. Lipid bodies may have provided sheltering envelopes for information storage, allowing the evolution of information-storing polymers like RNA. Only one or two types of vesicle-forming amphiphiles have been studied.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Chen_2010-146) There is an enormous number of possible arrangements of lipid bilayer membranes, and those with the best reproductive characteristics would have converged toward a hypercycle reaction,[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-147)[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-148) a positive [feedback](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feedback "Feedback") composed of two mutual catalysts represented by a membrane site and a specific compound trapped in the vesicle. Such site/compound pairs are transmissible to the daughter vesicles, leading to the emergence of distinct [lineages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lineage_(evolution) "Lineage (evolution)") of vesicles, subject to [natural selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_selection "Natural selection").[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-149)

A [protocell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protocell "Protocell") is a self-organized, self-ordered, spherical collection of lipids proposed as a stepping-stone to life.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Chen_2010-146) A functional protocell has (as of 2014) not yet been achieved in a laboratory setting.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Exploring-150)[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Chen_2006-151)[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Discover_2004-152) Self-assembled [vesicles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vesicle_(biology_and_chemistry) "Vesicle (biology and chemistry)") are essential components of primitive cells.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Chen_2010-146) The theory of classical irreversible thermodynamics treats self-assembly under a generalized chemical potential within the framework of [dissipative systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissipative_system "Dissipative system").[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-153)[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-154)[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-155) The [second law of thermodynamics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics "Second law of thermodynamics") requires that overall [entropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entropy "Entropy") increases, yet life is distinguished by its great degree of organization. Therefore, a boundary is needed to separate ordered [life processes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolism "Metabolism") from chaotic non-living matter.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-SciAm_2007-156)

Irene Chen and [Jack W. Szostak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jack_W._Szostak "Jack W. Szostak") suggest that elementary protocells can give rise to cellular behaviors including primitive forms of differential reproduction, competition, and energy storage.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Chen_2006-151) Competition for membrane molecules would favor stabilized membranes, suggesting a selective advantage for cross-linked fatty acids and even modern [phospholipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phospholipid "Phospholipid").[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Chen_2006-151) Such [micro-encapsulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micro-encapsulation "Micro-encapsulation") would allow for metabolism within the membrane and the exchange of small molecules, while retaining large biomolecules inside. Such a membrane is needed for a cell to create its own [electrochemical gradient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient "Electrochemical gradient").[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-157)[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lane_2015-158) Fatty acid vesicles in conditions relevant to alkaline hydrothermal vents can be stabilized by isoprenoids which are synthesized by the formose reaction; the advantages and disadvantages of isoprenoids incorporated within the lipid bilayer in different microenvironments might have led to the divergence of the membranes of archaea and bacteria.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-159)

Vesicles can undergo an evolutionary process under pressure cycling conditions.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-mayer2018-160) Simulating the systemic environment in tectonic [fault zones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fault_(geology) "Fault (geology)") within the [Earth's crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_crust "Earth's crust"), pressure cycling leads to the periodic formation of vesicles.[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-periodic-161) Under the same conditions, random [peptide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peptide "Peptide") chains are formed and selected for their ability to integrate into the vesicle membrane. A further selection of the vesicles for stability potentially leads to functional peptide structures, increasing the survival rate of the vesicles.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-162)[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-163)[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-164)

Life requires a loss of entropy, or disorder, as molecules organize themselves into living matter. At the same time, the emergence of life is associated with the formation of structures beyond a certain threshold of [complexity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complexity "Complexity").[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-165) The emergence of life with increasing order and complexity does not contradict the second law of thermodynamics, which states that overall entropy never decreases, since a living organism creates order in some places (e.g. its living body) at the expense of an increase of entropy elsewhere (e.g. heat and waste production).[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AP-2018-166)[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-167)[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-168)

Multiple sources of energy were available for chemical reactions on the early Earth. Heat from [geothermal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geothermal_energy "Geothermal energy") processes is a standard energy source for chemistry. Other examples include sunlight, lightning,[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Follmann2009-60) atmospheric entries of micro-meteorites,[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Bar-Nun_Bar-Nun_Bauer_Sagan_1970-169) and implosion of bubbles in sea and ocean waves.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Anbar_1968-170) This has been confirmed by experiments[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Dharmarathne_Grieser_2016-171)[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Patehebieke_Zhao_Wang_Xu_2021-172) and simulations.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Kalson_Furman_Zeiri_2017-173) Unfavorable reactions can be driven by highly favorable ones, as in the case of iron-sulfur chemistry. For example, this was probably important for [carbon fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fixation "Carbon fixation").[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-174) Carbon fixation by reaction of CO 2 with H 2 S via iron-sulfur chemistry is favorable, and occurs at neutral pH and 100°C. Iron-sulfur surfaces, which are abundant near hydrothermal vents, can drive the production of small amounts of amino acids and other biomolecules.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Follmann2009-60)

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/ATP-Synthase.svg/250px-ATP-Synthase.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ATP-Synthase.svg)

[ATP synthase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ATP_synthase "ATP synthase") uses the chemiosmotic proton gradient to power ATP synthesis through [oxidative phosphorylation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation "Oxidative phosphorylation").

In 1961, [Peter Mitchell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_D._Mitchell "Peter D. Mitchell") proposed [chemiosmosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemiosmosis "Chemiosmosis") as a cell's primary system of energy conversion. The mechanism, now ubiquitous in living cells, powers energy conversion in micro-organisms and in the [mitochondria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondria "Mitochondria") of eukaryotes, making it a likely candidate for early life.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-175)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-176) Mitochondria produce [adenosine triphosphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate "Adenosine triphosphate") (ATP), the energy currency of the cell used to drive cellular processes such as chemical syntheses. The mechanism of ATP synthesis involves a closed membrane in which the [ATP synthase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ATP_synthase "ATP synthase") enzyme is embedded. The energy required to release strongly bound ATP has its origin in [protons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton "Proton") that move across the membrane.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Junge_Nelson_2015-177) In modern cells, those proton movements are caused by the pumping of ions across the membrane, maintaining an electrochemical gradient. In the first organisms, the gradient could have been provided by the difference in chemical composition between the flow from a hydrothermal vent and the surrounding seawater,[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lane_2015-158) or perhaps meteoric quinones that were conducive to the development of chemiosmotic energy across lipid membranes if at a terrestrial origin.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Damer_2020-178)

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg/500px-Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Chemiosmotic_coupling_mitochondrion.svg)

[Chemiosmotic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemiosmosis "Chemiosmosis") coupling in the membranes of a [mitochondrion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondrion "Mitochondrion")

### PAH world hypothesis

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: PAH world hypothesis")]

The [PAH world hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PAH_world_hypothesis "PAH world hypothesis") is a speculative [hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypothesis "Hypothesis") that proposes that [polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon "Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon") (PAHs), known to be abundant in the [universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe"),[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-SP-20051018-109)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-AJ-20051010-107)[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-179) including in comets,[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NASA-20150210-180) and assumed to be abundant in the [primordial soup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primordial_soup#%22Primordial_soup%22_theory "Primordial soup") of the early [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth"), played a major role in the [origin of life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_life "Origin of life") by mediating the synthesis of [RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA "RNA") molecules, leading into the [RNA world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_world "RNA world"). However, as yet, the hypothesis is untested.[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-181)[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-182)

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Etls-2019-0024c.01.png/960px-Etls-2019-0024c.01.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Etls-2019-0024c.01.png)

The RNA world hypothesis proposes that undirected polymerisation led to the emergence of [ribozymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribozyme "Ribozyme"), and in turn to an [RNA replicase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase "RNA-dependent RNA polymerase").

The [RNA world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_world "RNA world") hypothesis describes an early Earth with self-replicating and catalytic RNA but no DNA or proteins.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-183) Many researchers concur that an RNA world must have preceded modern DNA-based life.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-184)[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-185)[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-186)[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-187) However, RNA-based life may not have been the first to exist.[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Robertson2012-188)[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Cech2012-189) Another model echoes Darwin's "warm little pond" with cycles of wetting and drying.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-190) Some have proposed a timeline of more than 30 potential significant chemical events between pre-RNA world to near but before LUCA, just involving RNA. The timeline does not include metabolism-related events such as origins of ATP, glycolysis, the Krebs cycle, and the electron transport chain.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-191)

RNA is central to the translation process. Small RNAs can catalyze all the chemical groups and information transfers required for life.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Cech2012-189)[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-192) RNA both expresses and maintains genetic information in modern organisms; and the chemical components of RNA are easily synthesized under conditions that approximate the early Earth. The structure of the [ribosome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribosome "Ribosome") has been called the "smoking gun", with a central core of RNA and no amino acid side chains within 18 [Å](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angstrom "Angstrom") of the [active site](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Active_site "Active site") that catalyzes peptide bond formation.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-193)[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Robertson2012-188)[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-194)

The concept of the RNA world was proposed in 1962 by [Alexander Rich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Rich "Alexander Rich"),[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-195) and the term was coined by [Walter Gilbert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Gilbert "Walter Gilbert") in 1986.[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Cech2012-189)[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-196) Initially it was hard to explain abiotic synthesis of the nucleotides cytosine and uracil.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-197) Subsequent research has shown possible routes of synthesis; for example, formamide produces all four [ribonucleotides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribonucleotide "Ribonucleotide") and other biological molecules when warmed in the presence of terrestrial minerals.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Saladino2012-125)[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Saladino2012b-126)

[RNA replicase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase "RNA-dependent RNA polymerase") can function as both code and catalyst for further RNA replication, i.e. it can be autocatalytic. [Jack Szostak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jack_Szostak "Jack Szostak") has shown that certain catalytic RNAs can link smaller RNA sequences together, enabling self-replication.
