RNA replication systems, which include two ribozymes that catalyze each other's synthesis, had a doubling time of about one hour, and were subject to natural selection.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-198)[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-199)[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Robertson2012-188) If such conditions were present on early Earth, then natural selection would favor the proliferation of such [autocatalytic sets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocatalytic_set "Autocatalytic set"), to which further functionalities could be added.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-200)[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-201)[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-202) Self-assembly of RNA may occur spontaneously in hydrothermal vents.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-203)[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-204)[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-205) A preliminary form of tRNA could have assembled into such a replicator molecule.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-206) When such an [RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA "RNA") molecule began to replicate, it may it may have been capable of the three mechanisms of Darwinian selection: [heritability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heritability "Heritability"), variation of type, and differential reproductive output. The fitness of such an RNA replicator (its per capita rate of increase) would likely have been a function of its intrinsic adaptive capabilities determined by its [nucleotide sequence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleic_acid_sequence "Nucleic acid sequence"), and the availability of resources.[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Bernstein1983-207)[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Michod2006-208)

Possible precursors to protein synthesis include the synthesis of short peptide cofactors or the self-catalysing duplication of RNA. It is likely that the ancestral ribosome was composed entirely of RNA, although some roles have since been taken over by proteins. Major remaining questions on this topic include identifying the selective force for the evolution of the ribosome and determining how the genetic code arose.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Noller2012-209)

[Eugene Koonin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eugene_Koonin "Eugene Koonin") has argued that "no compelling scenarios currently exist for the origin of replication and translation, the key processes that together comprise the core of biological systems and the apparent pre-requisite of biological evolution. The RNA World concept might offer the best chance for the resolution of this conundrum but so far cannot adequately account for the emergence of an efficient RNA replicase or the translation system."[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-pmc1892545-210)

### From RNA to directed protein synthesis

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: From RNA to directed protein synthesis")]

In line with the RNA world hypothesis, much of modern biology's templated protein biosynthesis is done by RNA molecules—namely tRNAs and the ribosome (consisting of both protein and rRNA components). The most central reaction of peptide bond synthesis is understood to be carried out by base catalysis by the 23S rRNA domain V.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-:1-211) Experimental evidence has demonstrated successful di- and tripeptide synthesis with a system consisting of only aminoacyl phosphate adaptors and RNA guides, which could be a possible stepping stone between an RNA world and modern protein synthesis.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-:1-211)[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-212) Aminoacylation ribozymes that can charge tRNAs with their cognate amino acids have also been selected in in vitro experimentation.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-:2-213) The authors also extensively mapped fitness landscapes within their selection to find that chance emergence of active sequences was more important that sequence optimization.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-:2-213)

### Early functional peptides

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Early functional peptides")]

The first proteins would have had to arise without a fully-fledged system of protein biosynthesis. As discussed above, numerous mechanisms for the prebiotic synthesis of polypeptides exist. However, these random sequence peptides would not have likely had biological function. Thus, significant study has gone into exploring how early functional proteins could have arisen from random sequences. First, some evidence on hydrolysis rates shows that abiotically plausible peptides likely contained significant "nearest-neighbor" biases.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-214) This could have had some effect on early protein sequence diversity. In other work by Anthony Keefe and Jack Szostak, [mRNA display](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MRNA_display "MRNA display") selection on a library of 6×10 12 80-mers was used to search for sequences with ATP binding activity. They concluded that approximately 1 in 10 11 random sequences had ATP binding function.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-215) While this is a single example of functional frequency in the random sequence space, the methodology can serve as a powerful simulation tool for understanding early protein evolution.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-216)

Starting with the work of [Carl Woese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Woese "Carl Woese") from 1977, [genomics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genomics "Genomics") studies have placed the last universal common ancestor (LUCA) of all modern life-forms between Bacteria and a clade formed by Archaea and [Eukaryota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryota "Eukaryota") in the phylogenetic tree of life. It lived over 4 Gya.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-217)[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Woese_Fox_1977-218) A minority of studies have placed the LUCA in Bacteria, proposing that Archaea and Eukaryota are evolutionarily derived from within Eubacteria;[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-219)[Thomas Cavalier-Smith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Cavalier-Smith "Thomas Cavalier-Smith") suggested in 2006 that the phenotypically diverse bacterial phylum [Chloroflexota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chloroflexota "Chloroflexota") contained the LUCA.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-CS2-220)

*   [![Image 12: Phylogenetic tree showing the last universal common ancestor (LUCA) at the root. The major clades are the Bacteria on one hand, and the Archaea and Eukaryota on the other.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg/960px-Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Phylogenetic_tree_of_life_LUCA.svg "Phylogenetic tree showing the last universal common ancestor (LUCA) at the root. The major clades are the Bacteria on one hand, and the Archaea and Eukaryota on the other.")

[Phylogenetic tree](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree "Phylogenetic tree") showing the [last universal common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") (LUCA) at the root. The major clades are the [Bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria") on one hand, and the [Archaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea "Archaea") and [Eukaryota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryota "Eukaryota") on the other. 

In 2016, a set of 355 genes likely present in the LUCA was identified. A total of 6.1 million prokaryotic genes from Bacteria and Archaea were sequenced, identifying 355 protein clusters from among 286,514 protein clusters that were probably common to the LUCA. The results suggest that the LUCA was [anaerobic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaerobic_organism "Anaerobic organism") with a Wood–Ljungdahl (reductive Acetyl-CoA) pathway, nitrogen- and carbon-fixing, thermophilic. Its [cofactors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry) "Cofactor (biochemistry)") suggest dependence upon an environment rich in hydrogen, carbon dioxide, iron, and [transition metals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transition_metal "Transition metal"). Its genetic material was probably DNA, requiring the 4-nucleotide genetic code, messenger RNA, transfer RNA, and ribosomes to translate the code into proteins such as enzymes. LUCA likely inhabited an anaerobic hydrothermal vent setting in a geochemically active environment. It was evidently already a complex organism, and must have had precursors; it was not the first living thing.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Weiss_Sousa_Mrnjavac_2016-10)[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Nature_2016-221) The physiology of LUCA has been in dispute.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-222)[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-223)[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-224) Previous research identified 60 proteins common to all life.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-225)

*   [![Image 13: LUCA systems and environment included the Wood–Ljungdahl pathway.[10]](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg/960px-LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:LUCA_systems_and_environment.svg "LUCA systems and environment included the Wood–Ljungdahl pathway.[10]")

LUCA systems and environment included the [Wood–Ljungdahl pathway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wood%E2%80%93Ljungdahl_pathway "Wood–Ljungdahl pathway").[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Weiss_Sousa_Mrnjavac_2016-10) 

Leslie Orgel argued that early translation machinery for the genetic code would be susceptible to [error catastrophe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Error_catastrophe "Error catastrophe"). Geoffrey Hoffmann however showed that such machinery can be stable in function against "Orgel's paradox".[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-226)[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-227)[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-228) Metabolic reactions that have also been inferred in LUCA are the incomplete [reverse Krebs cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reverse_Krebs_cycle "Reverse Krebs cycle"), [gluconeogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gluconeogenesis "Gluconeogenesis"), the [pentose phosphate pathway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentose_phosphate_pathway "Pentose phosphate pathway"), [glycolysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glycolysis "Glycolysis"), [reductive amination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reductive_amination "Reductive amination"), and [transamination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transamination "Transamination").[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-229)[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-230)

Suitable geological environments
--------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Suitable geological environments")]

A variety of [geologic and environmental settings have been proposed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternative_abiogenesis_scenarios "Alternative abiogenesis scenarios") for an origin of life. These theories are often in competition with one another as there are many views of prebiotic compound availability, geophysical setting, and early life characteristics. The first organism on Earth likely differed from [LUCA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor"). Between the first appearance of life and where all modern phylogenies began branching, an unknown amount of time passed, with unknown gene transfers, extinctions, and adaptation to environmental niches.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-231) Modern phylogenies provide more genetic evidence about LUCA than about its precursors.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-232)

### Deep sea hydrothermal vents

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Deep sea hydrothermal vents")]

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg/330px-Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Champagne_vent_white_smokers.jpg)

The [earliest known life forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms "Earliest known life forms") may be putative fossilized [microorganisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microorganism "Microorganism"), found in white smoker [hydrothermal vent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent "Hydrothermal vent") precipitates. They may have lived as early as 4.28 Gya (billion years ago), relatively soon after the [formation of the oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#History_of_water_on_Earth "Origin of water on Earth") 4.41 Gya, not long after the [formation of the Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth "Age of the Earth") 4.54 Gya.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-NAT-20170301-78)

Early micro-fossils may have come from a hot world of gases such as methane, ammonia, carbon dioxide, and [hydrogen sulfide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide "Hydrogen sulfide"), toxic to much current life.[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-233) Analysis of the [tree of life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tree_of_life_(biology) "Tree of life (biology)") places thermophilic and hyperthermophilic bacteria and archaea closest to the root, suggesting that life may have evolved in a hot environment.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-234) The deep sea or alkaline hydrothermal vent theory posits that life began at submarine hydrothermal vents.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Col%C3%ADn-Garc%C3%ADa_2016-235)[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-hydrothermal_vents_NASA_2014-236)[William Martin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_F._Martin "William F. Martin") and [Michael Russell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Russell_(scientist) "Michael Russell (scientist)") have suggested

> that life evolved in structured iron monosulphide precipitates in a seepage site hydrothermal mound at a redox, pH, and temperature gradient between sulphide-rich hydrothermal fluid and iron(II)-containing waters of the Hadean ocean floor. The naturally arising, three-dimensional compartmentation observed within fossilized seepage-site metal sulphide precipitates indicates that these inorganic compartments were the precursors of cell walls and membranes found in free-living prokaryotes. The known capability of FeS and NiS to catalyze the synthesis of the acetyl-methylsulphide from carbon monoxide and methylsulphide, constituents of hydrothermal fluid, indicates that pre-biotic syntheses occurred at the inner surfaces of these metal-sulphide-walled compartments.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Martin2003-41)

These form where hydrogen-rich fluids emerge from below the sea floor, as a result of [serpentinization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serpentinite "Serpentinite") of ultra-[mafic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mafic "Mafic")[olivine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olivine "Olivine") with seawater and a pH interface with carbon dioxide-rich ocean water. The vents form a sustained chemical energy source derived from redox reactions, in which electron donors (molecular hydrogen) react with electron acceptors (carbon dioxide); see [iron–sulfur world theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93sulfur_world_theory "Iron–sulfur world theory"). These are [exothermic reactions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exothermic_reaction "Exothermic reaction").[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Col%C3%ADn-Garc%C3%ADa_2016-235)[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-237)

#### Chemiosmotic gradient

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Chemiosmotic gradient")]

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg/500px-Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Leaky_membrane_cell_powered_by_external_proton_gradient.svg)

Proposed model of an early cell powered by external proton gradient near a deep-sea hydrothermal vent. As long as the membrane (or passive ion channels within it) is permeable to protons, the mechanism can function without ion pumps.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lane_2015-158)

Russell demonstrated that alkaline vents create an abiogenic [proton motive force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton_electromotive_force "Proton electromotive force") chemiosmotic gradient,[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Martin2003-41) ideal for abiogenesis. Their microscopic compartments "provide a natural means of concentrating organic molecules," composed of iron-sulfur minerals such as [mackinawite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mackinawite "Mackinawite"), endowed these mineral cells with the catalytic properties envisaged by [Günter Wächtershäuser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%BCnter_W%C3%A4chtersh%C3%A4user "Günter Wächtershäuser").[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lane_2009-238) This movement of ions across the membrane depends on two factors:[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lane_2015-158)

1.   [Diffusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffusion "Diffusion") force caused by concentration gradient—all particles including ions diffuse from higher concentration to lower.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lane_2015-158)
2.   Electrostatic force caused by electrical potential gradient—[cations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cation "Cation") like [protons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton "Proton") H+ diffuse down the electrical potential, [anions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anion "Anion") in the opposite direction.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lane_2015-158)

These two gradients together can be expressed as an electrochemical gradient, providing energy for abiogenic synthesis. The proton motive force measures the potential energy stored as proton and voltage gradients across a membrane (differences in proton concentration and electrical potential).[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Lane_2015-158)

The surfaces of mineral particles inside deep-ocean hydrothermal vents have catalytic properties similar to those of enzymes, and can create simple organic molecules, such as [methanol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanol "Methanol") (CH 3 OH) and [formic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formic_acid "Formic acid"), [acetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acetic_acid "Acetic acid"), and [pyruvic acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyruvic_acid "Pyruvic acid") out of the dissolved CO 2 in the water, if driven by an applied voltage or by reaction with H 2 or H 2 S.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-organics-239)[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-240)

Starting in 1985, researchers proposed that life arose at hydrothermal vents,[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-241)[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-242) that spontaneous chemistry in the Earth's crust driven by rock–water interactions at disequilibrium thermodynamically underpinned life's origin,[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-243)[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-244) and that the founding lineages of the archaea and bacteria were H 2-dependent autotrophs that used CO 2 as their terminal acceptor in energy metabolism.[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-245) In 2016, Martin suggested that the LUCA "may have depended heavily on the geothermal energy of the vent to survive".[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Weiss_Sousa_Mrnjavac_2016-10) That same year, RNA was produced in synthetic alkaline hydrothermal chimneys inside a laboratory setting which replicated deep-sea vents.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-246) Pores at deep sea hydrothermal vents are suggested to have been occupied by membrane-bound compartments which promoted biochemical reactions.[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-247)[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-248) Metabolic intermediates in the Krebs cycle, gluconeogenesis, amino acid bio-synthetic pathways, glycolysis, the pentose phosphate pathway, and including sugars like ribose, and lipid precursors can occur non-enzymatically at conditions relevant to deep-sea alkaline hydrothermal vents.[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-249)

If the deep marine hydrothermal setting was the site, then abiogenesis could have happened as early as 4.0–4.2 Gya. If life evolved in the ocean at depths of more than ten meters, it would have been shielded both from impacts and the then high levels of solar ultraviolet radiation. The available energy in hydrothermal vents is maximized at 100–150°C, the temperatures at which [hyperthermophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperthermophile "Hyperthermophile") bacteria and [thermoacidophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoacidophile "Thermoacidophile")[archaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea "Archaea") live.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-woese-250)[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-251) Arguments against a hydrothermal origin of life state that hyperthermophily was a result of [convergent evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_evolution "Convergent evolution") in bacteria and archaea, and that a [mesophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesophile "Mesophile") environment would have been more likely.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-252)[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-science.org-253) This hypothesis, suggested in 1999 by Galtier, was proposed one year before the discovery of the Lost City Hydrothermal Field, where white-smoker hydrothermal vents average ≈45–90°C.[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-254)

#### Arguments against a vent setting

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Arguments against a vent setting")]

Production of prebiotic organic compounds at hydrothermal vents is estimated to be 10 8 kg/[yr](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Year#Symbols_and_abbreviations "Year").[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Ehrenfreund_Irvine_Becker_2002-255) While a large amount of key prebiotic compounds, such as methane, are found at vents, they are in far lower concentrations than in a Miller-Urey Experiment environment. In the case of methane, the production rate at vents is 2–4 orders of magnitude lower than in a [Miller-Urey Experiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment "Miller–Urey experiment") surface atmosphere.[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Ehrenfreund_Irvine_Becker_2002-255)[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-256)

Other contra-arguments include the inability to concentrate prebiotic materials, due to strong dilution by seawater. This open system cycles compounds through vent minerals, leaving little residence time to accumulate.[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-257) All modern cells rely on phosphates and potassium for nucleotide backbone and protein formation respectively, making it likely that the first life forms shared these functions. These elements were not available in high quantities in the Archaean oceans, as both primarily come from the weathering of continental rocks on land, far from vents. Submarine hydrothermal vents are not conducive to condensation reactions needed for polymerisation of macromolecules.[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-258)[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-259)

An older argument was that key polymers were encapsulated in vesicles after condensation, which supposedly would not happen in saltwater. However, while salinity inhibits vesicle formation from low-diversity mixtures of fatty acids,[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Deamer_2021-260) vesicle formation from a broader, more realistic mix of fatty-acid and 1-alkanol species is more resilient.[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Jordan_2019-261)[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Deamer_2021-260)

### Surface bodies of water

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Surface bodies of water")]

Surface bodies of water provide environments that dry out and rewet. Wet-dry cycles concentrate prebiotic compounds and enable [condensation reactions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Condensation_reaction "Condensation reaction") to polymerise macromolecules. Moreover, lakes and ponds receive detrital input from weathering of continental [apatite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apatite "Apatite")-containing rocks, the most common source of phosphates. The amount of exposed continental crust in the Hadean is unknown, but models of early ocean depths and rates of ocean island and continental crust growth make it plausible that there was exposed land.[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-262) Another line of evidence for a surface start to life is the requirement for [Ultraviolet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet "Ultraviolet") radiation (UV) for organism function. UV is necessary for the formation of the U+C nucleotide [base pair](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_pair "Base pair") by partial [hydrolysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrolysis "Hydrolysis") and nucleobase loss.[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-263) Simultaneously, UV can be harmful and sterilising to life, especially for simple early lifeforms with little ability to repair radiation damage. Radiation levels from a young Sun were likely greater, and, with no [ozone layer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone_layer "Ozone layer"), harmful shortwave UV rays would reach the surface of Earth. For life to begin, a shielded environment with influx from UV-exposed sources is necessary to both benefit and protect from UV. Shielding under ice, liquid water, mineral surfaces (e.g. clay) or regolith is possible in a range of surface water settings.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-264)

Most branching phylogenies are thermophilic or hyperthermophilic, making it possible that LUCA and preceding lifeforms were similarly thermophilic. Hot springs are formed from the heating of groundwater by geothermal activity. This intersection allows for influxes of material from deep penetrating waters and from surface runoff that transports eroded continental sediments. Interconnected groundwater systems create a mechanism for distribution of life to wider area.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-woese-250)

Mulkidjanian and co-authors argue that marine environments did not provide the ionic balance and composition universally found in cells, or the ions required by essential proteins and ribozymes, especially with respect to high K+/Na+ ratio, Mn 2+, Zn 2+ and phosphate concentrations. They argue that the only environments that do this are hot springs similar to ones at Kamchatka.[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Mulkidjanian_Bychkov_2012-265) Mineral deposits in these environments under an anoxic atmosphere would have suitable pH, contain precipitates of photocatalytic sulfide minerals that absorb harmful ultraviolet radiation, and have wet-dry cycles that concentrate substrate solutions enough for spontaneous formation of biopolymers[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-266)[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-267) created both by chemical reactions in the hydrothermal environment, and by exposure to [UV light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UV_light "UV light") during transport from vents to adjacent pools.[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-268) The hypothesized pre-biotic environments are similar to hydrothermal vents, with additional components that help explain peculiarities of the LUCA.[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Mulkidjanian_Bychkov_2012-265)[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Damer_2020-178)

A phylogenomic and geochemical analysis of proteins plausibly traced to the LUCA shows that the ionic composition of its intracellular fluid is identical to that of hot springs. The LUCA likely was dependent upon synthesized organic matter for its growth.[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Mulkidjanian_Bychkov_2012-265) Experiments show that RNA-like polymers can be synthesized in wet-dry cycling and UV light exposure. These polymers were encapsulated in vesicles after condensation.[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Deamer_2021-260) Potential sources of organics at hot springs might have been transport by interplanetary dust particles, extraterrestrial projectiles, or atmospheric or geochemical synthesis. Hot springs could have been abundant in volcanic landmasses during the Hadean.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Damer_2020-178)

#### Temperate surface bodies of water

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Temperate surface bodies of water")]

A [mesophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesophile "Mesophile") start in surface bodies of waters hypothesis has evolved from Darwin's concept of a '[warm little pond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warm_little_pond "Warm little pond")' and the [Oparin-Haldane hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primordial_soup "Primordial soup"). Freshwater bodies under temperate climates can accumulate prebiotic materials while providing suitable environmental conditions conducive to simple life forms. The Archaean climate is uncertain. Atmospheric reconstructions from geochemical proxies and models suggest that sufficient greenhouse gases were present to maintain surface temperatures between 0–40°C. If so, the temperature was suitable for life could begin.[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-269)

Strong evidence for mesophily from biomolecular studies includes Galtier's [G+C](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GC-content "GC-content") nucleotide thermometer. G+C are more abundant in thermophiles due to the added stability of an additional hydrogen bond not present between A+T nucleotides. [rRNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA "Ribosomal RNA") sequencing on a diverse range of modern lifeforms shows that [LUCA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor")'s reconstructed G+C content was likely representative of moderate temperatures.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-science.org-253)

It is possible that the diversity of thermophiles today is a product of convergent evolution and horizontal gene transfer rather than an inherited trait from LUCA.[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-270) The [reverse gyrase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reverse_gyrase "Reverse gyrase")[topoisomerase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topoisomerase "Topoisomerase") is found exclusively in thermophiles and hyperthermophiles as it allows for coiling of DNA.[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-271) This enzyme requires the complex molecule [ATP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ATP_synthase "ATP synthase") to function. If an origin of life is hypothesised to involve a simple organism that had not yet evolved a membrane, let alone ATP, this would make the existence of reverse gyrase improbable. Moreover, phylogenetic studies show that reverse gyrase had an archaeal origin, and transferred to bacteria by horizontal gene transfer, implying it was not present in the LUCA.[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-272)

#### Icy surface bodies of water

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Icy surface bodies of water")]

Cold-start theories presuppose large ice-covered regions. Stellar evolution models predict that the Sun's luminosity was ≈25% weaker than it is today. Fuelner states that although this significant decrease in solar energy would have formed an icy planet, there is strong evidence for the presence of liquid water, possibly driven by a greenhouse effect. This would mean an early Earth with both liquid oceans and icy poles.[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-273)

Ice melts that form from ice sheets or glacier melts create freshwater pools, another niche capable of wet-dry cycles. While surface pools would be exposed to intense UV radiation, bodies of water within and under ice would be shielded, while remaining connected to exposed areas through ice cracks. Impact melting would allow freshwater and meteoritic input, creating prebiotic components.[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-274) Near-seawater levels of sodium chloride destabilize fatty acid membrane self-assembly, making freshwater settings appealing for early membranous life.[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-275)

Icy environments would trade the faster reaction rates that occur in warm environments for increased stability and accumulation of larger polymers.[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-276) Experiments simulating Europa-like conditions of ≈20°C have synthesised amino acids and adenine, showing that Miller-Urey type syntheses can occur at low temperatures.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-jeffreybada-134) In an [RNA world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_world "RNA world"), the ribozyme would have had even more functions than in a later DNA-RNA-protein-world. For RNA to function, it must be able to fold, a process hindered by temperatures above 30°C. While RNA folding in [psychrophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Psychrophile "Psychrophile") organisms is slower, so is hydrolysis, so folding is more successful. Shorter nucleotides would not suffer from higher temperatures.[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-277)[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-278)

### Inside the continental crust

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Abiogenesis&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Inside the continental crust")]

An alternative geological environment has been proposed by the geologist Ulrich Schreiber and the physical chemist Christian Mayer: the [continental crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continental_crust "Continental crust").[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-279)[Tectonic fault](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fault_(geology) "Fault (geology)") zones could present a stable and well-protected environment for long-term prebiotic evolution. Inside these systems of cracks and cavities, water and carbon dioxide present the bulk solvents. Their phase state would depend on the local temperature and pressure conditions and could vary between liquid, gaseous and [supercritical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercritical_fluid "Supercritical fluid"). When forming two separate phases (e.g., liquid water and supercritical carbon dioxide in depths of little more than 1 km), the system provides optimal conditions for [phase transfer reactions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phase-transfer_catalyst "Phase-transfer catalyst"). Concurrently, the contents of the tectonic fault zones are being supplied by a multitude of inorganic educts (e.g., carbon monoxide, hydrogen, ammonia, hydrogen cyanide, nitrogen, and even phosphate from dissolved apatite) and simple organic molecules formed by hydrothermal chemistry (e.g. amino acids, long-chain amines, fatty acids, long-chain aldehydes).[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-280)[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-281)

An especially interesting section of the tectonic fault zones is located at a depth of approximately 1000 m. For the carbon dioxide part of the bulk solvent, it provides temperature and pressure conditions near the [phase transition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phase_transition "Phase transition") point between the [supercritical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercritical_fluid "Supercritical fluid") and the gaseous state. This leads to a natural accumulation zone for [lipophilic organic molecules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipophilicity "Lipophilicity") that dissolve well in [supercritical CO 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide "Supercritical carbon dioxide"), but not in its gaseous state, leading to their local precipitation.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-282) Periodic pressure variations such as caused by [geyser activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geyser "Geyser") or [tidal influences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_force "Tidal force") result in periodic phase transitions, keeping the local reaction environment in a constant [non-equilibrium state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-equilibrium_thermodynamics "Non-equilibrium thermodynamics"). In presence of [amphiphilic compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amphiphile "Amphiphile") (such as the long chain amines and fatty acids mentioned above), subsequent generations of vesicles are being formed[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-periodic-161) that are constantly and efficiently being selected for their stability.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-mayer2018-160)

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png/250px-Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Glutamic-acid-from-xtal-view-2-3D-bs-17.png)

Many biomolecules, such as [L-glutamic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glutamic_acid "Glutamic acid"), are [asymmetric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chirality "Chirality"), and occur in living systems in only one of the two possible forms, in the case of [amino acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amino_acid "Amino acid") the left-handed form. Prebiotic chemistry would produce both forms, creating a puzzle for abiogenesis researchers.[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Plasson2007-283)

Homochirality is the geometric uniformity of materials composed of [chiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chirality "Chirality") (non-mirror-symmetric) units. Living organisms use molecules that have the same chirality (handedness): with almost no exceptions,[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-284) amino acids are left-handed while nucleotides and [sugars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbohydrate "Carbohydrate") are right-handed. Chiral molecules can be synthesized, but in the absence of a chiral source or a chiral catalyst, they are formed in a 50/50 (racemic) mixture of both [forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enantiomer "Enantiomer"). Known mechanisms for the production of non-racemic mixtures from racemic starting materials include: asymmetric physical laws, such as the [electroweak interaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electroweak_interaction "Electroweak interaction"); asymmetric environments, such as those caused by [circularly polarized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circular_polarization "Circular polarization") light, [quartz crystals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quartz "Quartz"), or the Earth's rotation, [statistical fluctuations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistical_fluctuations "Statistical fluctuations") during racemic synthesis,[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Plasson2007-283) and [spontaneous symmetry breaking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spontaneous_symmetry_breaking "Spontaneous symmetry breaking").[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-jafarpour2017-285)[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-jafarpour2015-286)[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-frank1953-287)

Once established, chirality would be selected for.[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-288) A small bias ([enantiomeric excess](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enantiomeric_excess "Enantiomeric excess")) in the population can be amplified into a large one by [asymmetric autocatalysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autocatalysis#Asymmetric_autocatalysis "Autocatalysis"), such as in the [Soai reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soai_reaction "Soai reaction").[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-289) In asymmetric autocatalysis, the catalyst is a chiral molecule, which means that a chiral molecule is catalyzing its own production. An initial enantiomeric excess, such as can be produced by polarized light, then allows the more abundant enantiomer to outcompete the other.[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Soai2001-290)

Homochirality may have started in outer space: on the [Murchison meteorite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murchison_meteorite "Murchison meteorite"), the left-handed amino acid [L-alanine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alanine "Alanine") is more than twice as frequent as its right-handed D form, and [L-glutamic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glutamic_acid "Glutamic acid") is more than three times as abundant as its D counterpart.[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-291)[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-Meierhenrich-292) Amino acids from meteorites show a left-handed bias, whereas sugars show a predominantly right-handed bias: this is the same preference found in living organisms, suggesting an abiogenic origin of these compounds.[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-StarStuff-293)

In a 2010 experiment by Robert Root-Bernstein, "two D-RNA-oligonucleotides having inverse base sequences (D-CGUA and D-AUGC) and their corresponding L-RNA-oligonucleotides (L-CGUA and L-AUGC) were synthesized and their affinity determined for Gly and eleven pairs of L- and D-amino acids". The results suggest that homochirality, including codon directionality, might have "emerged as a function of the origin of the genetic code".[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_note-294)

2.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis#cite_ref-237)**The reactions are:

**Reaction 1**: Fayalite + water → magnetite + aqueous silica + hydrogen 3Fe 2 SiO 4 + 2H 2 O → 2Fe 3 O 4 + 3SiO 2 + 2H 2**Reaction 2**: Forsterite + aqueous silica → serpentine 3Mg 2 SiO 4 + SiO 2 + 4H 2 O → 2Mg 3 Si 2 O 5(OH)4**Reaction 3**: Forsterite + water → serpentine + brucite 2Mg 2 SiO 4 + 3H 2 O → Mg 3 Si 2 O 5(OH)4 + Mg(OH)2 Reaction 3 describes the hydration of olivine with water only to yield [serpentine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serpentine_group "Serpentine group") and Mg(OH)2 ([brucite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brucite "Brucite")). Serpentine is stable at high pH in the presence of brucite like calcium silicate hydrate, ([C-S-H](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calcium_silicate_hydrate "Calcium silicate hydrate")) phases formed along with [portlandite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portlandite "Portlandite") (Ca(OH)2) in hardened [Portland cement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portland_cement "Portland cement") paste after the hydration of [belite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belite "Belite") (Ca 2 SiO 4), the artificial calcium equivalent of forsterite. Analogy of reaction 3 with belite hydration in ordinary Portland cement: _Belite + water → C-S-H phase + portlandite_ 2 Ca 2 SiO 4 + 4 H 2 O → 3 CaO · 2 SiO 2 · 3 H 2 O + Ca(OH)2

Thomas Huxley (1825–1895) used the term abiogenesis in an important text published in 1870. He strictly made the difference between spontaneous generation, which he did not accept, and the possibility of the evolution of matter from inert to living, without any influence of life. ... Since the end of the nineteenth century, evolutive abiogenesis means increasing complexity and evolution of matter from inert to living state in the abiotic context of evolution of primitive Earth.
